	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Iron Maiden</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the band. For their self-titled album, see <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden_(album)" title="Iron Maiden (album)">Iron Maiden (album)</a>. <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">English heavy metal band</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

<p><b>Iron Maiden</b> are an English <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal</a> band formed in <a href="/wiki/Leyton" title="Leyton">Leyton</a>, East London, in 1975 by bassist and primary songwriter <a href="/wiki/Steve_Harris_(musician)" title="Steve Harris (musician)">Steve Harris</a>. The band's <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden_discography" title="Iron Maiden discography">discography</a> has grown to thirty-nine albums, including sixteen studio albums, twelve live albums, four <a href="/wiki/Extended_play" title="Extended play">EPs</a>, and seven <a href="/wiki/Compilation_album" title="Compilation album">compilations</a>.
</p><p>Pioneers of the <a href="/wiki/New_wave_of_British_heavy_metal" title="New wave of British heavy metal">new wave of British heavy metal</a>, Iron Maiden achieved initial success during the early 1980s. After several line-up changes, the band went on to release a series of UK and US platinum and gold albums, including 1982's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Number_of_the_Beast_(album)" title="The Number of the Beast (album)">The Number of the Beast</a></i>, 1983's <i><a href="/wiki/Piece_of_Mind" title="Piece of Mind">Piece of Mind</a></i>, 1984's <i><a href="/wiki/Powerslave" title="Powerslave">Powerslave</a></i>, 1985's live release <i><a href="/wiki/Live_After_Death" title="Live After Death">Live After Death</a></i>, 1986's <i><a href="/wiki/Somewhere_in_Time_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Somewhere in Time (Iron Maiden album)">Somewhere in Time</a></i> and 1988's <i><a href="/wiki/Seventh_Son_of_a_Seventh_Son" title="Seventh Son of a Seventh Son">Seventh Son of a Seventh Son</a></i>. Since the return of lead vocalist <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Dickinson" title="Bruce Dickinson">Bruce Dickinson</a> and guitarist <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Smith" title="Adrian Smith">Adrian Smith</a> in 1999, the band has undergone a resurgence in popularity,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2010_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2010-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> with their 2010 studio offering, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Final_Frontier" title="The Final Frontier">The Final Frontier</a></i>, peaking at No.&#160;1 in 28&#160;countries and receiving widespread critical acclaim. Their sixteenth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Souls" title="The Book of Souls">The Book of Souls</a></i>, was released on 4 September 2015 to similar success.
</p><p>Despite little radio or television support,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2009_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2009-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Iron Maiden are considered one of the most successful heavy metal bands in history, with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">The Sunday Times</a></i> reporting in 2017 that the band have sold over 100&#160;million copies of their albums worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThring2017_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThring2017-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2017_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The band won the <a href="/wiki/Ivor_Novello_Awards" title="Ivor Novello Awards">Ivor Novello Award</a> for international achievement in 2002. As of October&#160;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the band have played over <a href="/wiki/List_of_Iron_Maiden_concert_tours" title="List of Iron Maiden concert tours">2000 live shows</a> throughout their career. For 40&#160;years the band have been supported by their famous mascot, "<a href="/wiki/Eddie_(mascot)" title="Eddie (mascot)">Eddie</a>", who has appeared on almost all of their album and single covers, as well as in their live shows.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Early_years_.281975.E2.80.931978.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years_(1975–1978)">Early years (1975–1978)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years (1975–1978)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cartandhorses.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cartandhorses.jpg/220px-Cartandhorses.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cartandhorses.jpg/330px-Cartandhorses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cartandhorses.jpg/440px-Cartandhorses.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2389" data-file-height="1317" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cartandhorses.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cart and Horses Pub, located in Maryland Point, Stratford, was where Iron Maiden played some of their first shows in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200429BushellHalfin19859_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200429BushellHalfin19859-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Iron Maiden were formed on Christmas Day, 25 December 1975 by bassist <a href="/wiki/Steve_Harris_(musician)" title="Steve Harris (musician)">Steve Harris</a> shortly after he left his previous group, Smiler. Harris attributed the band's name to a film adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_in_the_Iron_Mask_(1939_film)" title="The Man in the Iron Mask (1939 film)">The Man in the Iron Mask</a></i> from the novel by <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas" title="Alexandre Dumas">Alexandre Dumas</a>, the title of which reminded him of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_maiden" title="Iron maiden">iron maiden torture device</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarton1979_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarton1979-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> After months of rehearsal, Iron Maiden made their debut at St. Nicks Hall in Poplar on 1 May 1976,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEMI2004_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEMI2004-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> before taking up a semi-residency at the Cart and Horses Pub in <a href="/wiki/Maryland,_London" title="Maryland, London">Maryland</a>, Stratford.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200429_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200429-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The original line-up was short-lived, with vocalist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Day_(musician)" title="Paul Day (musician)">Paul Day</a> being the first casualty as, according to Harris, he lacked "energy or charisma on stage".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200432_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200432-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He was replaced by Dennis Wilcock, a <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a> fan who used make-up and fake blood during live performances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200432_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200432-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Wilcock's friend, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Murray_(musician)" title="Dave Murray (musician)">Dave Murray</a>, was invited to join, much to the dismay of the band's guitarists Dave Sullivan and Terry Rance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200433_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200433-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Their frustration led Harris to temporarily disband Iron Maiden in 1976,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200433_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200433-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> though the group reformed soon after with Murray as the sole guitarist. Harris and Murray remain the band's longest-standing members and have performed on all of their releases.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harris_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Harris_1.jpg/220px-Harris_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Harris_1.jpg/330px-Harris_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Harris_1.jpg/440px-Harris_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="573" data-file-height="408" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harris_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dave Murray and Steve Harris in 2008. Harris and Murray are the only members to have performed on all of the band's albums.</div></div></div>
<p>Iron Maiden recruited yet another guitarist in 1977, Bob Sawyer, who was sacked for embarrassing the band on stage by pretending to play guitar with his teeth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200444_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200444-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Tension ensued again, causing a rift between Murray and Wilcock, who convinced Harris to fire Murray,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200446_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200446-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> as well as original drummer Ron Matthews.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEMI2004_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEMI2004-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> A new line-up was put together, including future <a href="/wiki/Cutting_Crew" title="Cutting Crew">Cutting Crew</a> member <a href="/wiki/Tony_Moore_(musician)" title="Tony Moore (musician)">Tony Moore</a> on keyboards, Terry Wapram on guitar, and drummer <a href="/wiki/Thunderstick" title="Thunderstick">Barry Purkis</a> (better known today as Thunderstick). A poor performance at the Bridgehouse, a pub located in Canning Town,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200423_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200423-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> in November 1977 was the line-up's first and only concert. Afterwards, Iron Maiden fired Purkis and replaced him with <a href="/wiki/Doug_Sampson" title="Doug Sampson">Doug Sampson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200448_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200448-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, Moore was asked to leave as Harris decided that keyboards did not suit the band's sound.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200448_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200448-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> A few months later, Dennis Wilcock decided to leave Iron Maiden to form his own band, V1, and Dave Murray was immediately reinstated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200450_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200450-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> As he preferred to be the band's sole guitarist, Wapram disapproved of Murray's return, and was also dismissed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEMI2004_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEMI2004-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Harris, Murray, and Sampson spent the summer and autumn of 1978 rehearsing while they searched for a singer to complete the band's new line-up.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200452_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200452-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> A chance meeting at the Red Lion pub in <a href="/wiki/Leytonstone" title="Leytonstone">Leytonstone</a> in November 1978 evolved into a successful audition for vocalist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Di%27Anno" title="Paul Di&#39;Anno">Paul Di'Anno</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200454_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200454-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Steve Harris stated, "There's sort of a quality in Paul's voice, a raspiness in his voice, or whatever you want to call it, that just gave it this great edge."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200453_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200453-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> At this time, Murray would typically act as their sole guitarist, with Harris commenting, "Davey was so good he could do a lot of it on his own. The plan was always to get a second guitarist in, but finding one that could match Davey was really difficult."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200464_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200464-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Record_contract_and_early_releases_.281978.E2.80.931981.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Record_contract_and_early_releases_(1978–1981)">Record contract and early releases (1978–1981)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Record contract and early releases (1978–1981)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/The_Soundhouse_Tapes" title="The Soundhouse Tapes">The Soundhouse Tapes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden_(album)" title="Iron Maiden (album)">Iron Maiden (album)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Killers_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Killers (Iron Maiden album)">Killers (Iron Maiden album)</a></div>
<p>On New Year's Eve 1978, Iron Maiden recorded a demo, consisting of four songs, at Spaceward Studios in Cambridge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200466_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200466-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Hoping that the recording would help them secure more gigs,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200466_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200466-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> the band presented a copy to <a href="/wiki/Neal_Kay" title="Neal Kay">Neal Kay</a>, then managing a heavy metal club called "Bandwagon Heavy Metal Soundhouse", located in Kingsbury Circle, northwest London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200467_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200467-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Upon hearing the tape, Kay began playing the demo regularly at the Bandwagon, and one of the songs, "Prowler", eventually went to No.&#160;1 in the Soundhouse charts, which were published weekly in <i><a href="/wiki/Sounds_(magazine)" title="Sounds (magazine)">Sounds</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004104–105_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004104–105-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> A copy was also acquired by <a href="/wiki/Rod_Smallwood" title="Rod Smallwood">Rod Smallwood</a>, who soon became the band's manager,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall200483_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall200483-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and, as Iron Maiden's popularity increased, they released the demo on their own record label as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Soundhouse_Tapes" title="The Soundhouse Tapes">The Soundhouse Tapes</a></i>, named after the club.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004103_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004103-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Featuring only three tracks (one song, "Strange World", was excluded as the band were unsatisfied with its production)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004102_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004102-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> all five thousand copies were sold out within weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004104-105_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004104-105-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1979, the band secured a major record deal with <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004108_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004108-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and asked Dave Murray's childhood friend, <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Smith" title="Adrian Smith">Adrian Smith</a> of <a href="/wiki/Urchin_(band)" title="Urchin (band)">Urchin</a>, to join the group as their second guitarist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004163–164_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004163–164-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Due to his commitment to Urchin, Smith declined and <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Stratton" title="Dennis Stratton">Dennis Stratton</a> was hired instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004121_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004121-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, <a href="/wiki/Doug_Sampson" title="Doug Sampson">Doug Sampson</a> left due to health issues, and was replaced by ex-<a href="/wiki/Samson_(band)" title="Samson (band)">Samson</a> drummer <a href="/wiki/Clive_Burr" title="Clive Burr">Clive Burr</a> at Stratton's suggestion on 26 December 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004123_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004123-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Iron Maiden's first appearance on an album was on the <i><a href="/wiki/Metal_for_Muthas" title="Metal for Muthas">Metal for Muthas</a></i> compilation (released on 15 February 1980) with two early versions of "<a href="/wiki/Sanctuary_(Iron_Maiden_song)" title="Sanctuary (Iron Maiden song)">Sanctuary</a>" and "Wrathchild".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004137_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004137-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The release led to an <a href="/wiki/Metal_for_Muthas_Tour" title="Metal for Muthas Tour">ensuing tour</a> which featured several other bands linked with the <a href="/wiki/New_wave_of_British_heavy_metal" title="New wave of British heavy metal">new wave of British heavy metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004139Saulnier2012_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004139Saulnier2012-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg/170px-IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg/255px-IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg/340px-IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3522" data-file-height="4760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IRON_MAIDEN_-_Manchester_Apollo_-_1980.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paul_Di%27Anno" title="Paul Di&#39;Anno">Paul Di'Anno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Harris_(musician)" title="Steve Harris (musician)">Steve Harris</a> supporting <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">Judas Priest</a> on their British Steel Tour, 1980</div></div></div>
<p>Iron Maiden released their eponymous <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden_(album)" title="Iron Maiden (album)">self-titled album</a> in 1980, which debuted at No.&#160;4 in the <a href="/wiki/UK_Albums_Chart" title="UK Albums Chart">UK Albums Chart</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004143_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004143-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the title track (a live version of which would be one of the <a href="/wiki/First_music_videos_aired_on_MTV" class="mw-redirect" title="First music videos aired on MTV">first music videos aired on MTV</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoland2013_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoland2013-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> the album includes other early favourites such as "<a href="/wiki/Running_Free" title="Running Free">Running Free</a>", "Transylvania", "Phantom of the Opera", and "<a href="/wiki/Sanctuary_(Iron_Maiden_song)" title="Sanctuary (Iron Maiden song)">Sanctuary</a>" – which was not on the original UK release, but appeared on the US version and subsequent remasters. The band embarked on a <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden_Tour" title="Iron Maiden Tour">headline tour of the UK</a>, before opening for <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a> on their 1980 <a href="/wiki/Unmasked_Tour" title="Unmasked Tour">Unmasked Tour</a>'s European leg as well as supporting <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">Judas Priest</a> on <a href="/wiki/British_Steel_Tour" title="British Steel Tour">select dates</a>. Iron Maiden also appeared, to much acclaim, at the Reading Festival 1980. They were second to top of the bill on the Saturday, with UFO headlining. After the Kiss tour, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Stratton" title="Dennis Stratton">Dennis Stratton</a> was dismissed from the band as a result of creative and personal differences,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinchcliffe1999_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinchcliffe1999-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Smith" title="Adrian Smith">Adrian Smith</a> in October 1980.
</p><p>In 1981, Iron Maiden released their second studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Killers_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Killers (Iron Maiden album)">Killers</a></i>. Containing many tracks written prior to their debut release, only two new songs were written for the record: "Prodigal Son" and "Murders in the Rue Morgue"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004183_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004183-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> (the latter's title was taken from the <a href="/wiki/The_Murders_in_the_Rue_Morgue" title="The Murders in the Rue Morgue">short story</a> by <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrannigan_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrannigan-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Unsatisfied with the production on their debut album,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004145_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004145-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> the band hired veteran producer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Birch" title="Martin Birch">Martin Birch</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004181_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004181-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> who would go on to work for Iron Maiden until his retirement in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004300_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004300-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The record was followed by the band's first <a href="/wiki/Killer_World_Tour" title="Killer World Tour">world tour</a>, which included their debut performance in the United States, opening for Judas Priest at <a href="/wiki/Planet_Hollywood_Resort_and_Casino" class="mw-redirect" title="Planet Hollywood Resort and Casino">The Aladdin Casino</a> in Las Vegas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004251_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004251-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Success_.281981.E2.80.931985.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Success_(1981–1985)">Success (1981–1985)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Success (1981–1985)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/The_Number_of_the_Beast_(album)" title="The Number of the Beast (album)">The Number of the Beast (album)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piece_of_Mind" title="Piece of Mind">Piece of Mind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Powerslave" title="Powerslave">Powerslave</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Live_After_Death" title="Live After Death">Live After Death</a></div>
<p>By 1981, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Di%27Anno" title="Paul Di&#39;Anno">Paul Di'Anno</a> was demonstrating increasingly self-destructive behaviour, particularly due to his drug usage,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEMI2004_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEMI2004-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> about which Di'Anno comments, "it wasn't just that I was snorting a bit of coke, though; I was just going for it non-stop, 24 hours a day, every day&#160;... the band had commitments piling up that went on for months, years, and I just couldn't see my way to the end of it. I knew I'd never last the whole tour. It was too much."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004194_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004194-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> With his performances waning, Di'Anno was immediately dismissed following the Killer World Tour,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004219_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004219-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> at which point the band had already selected his replacement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004218_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004218-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After a meeting with Rod Smallwood at the <a href="/wiki/Reading_and_Leeds_Festivals" title="Reading and Leeds Festivals">Reading Festival</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004217_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004217-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Dickinson" title="Bruce Dickinson">Bruce Dickinson</a>, previously of <a href="/wiki/Samson_(band)" title="Samson (band)">Samson</a>, auditioned for Iron Maiden in September 1981 and was immediately hired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004218_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004218-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  The following month, Dickinson went out on the road with the band on a small headlining tour in Italy, as well as a one-off show at the <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_Theatre" title="Rainbow Theatre">Rainbow Theatre</a> in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004219_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004219-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> For the last show, and in anticipation of their forthcoming album, the band played "Children of the Damned" and "22 Acacia Avenue", introducing fans to the sound towards which they were progressing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDome2014_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDome2014-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1982, Iron Maiden released their third studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Number_of_the_Beast_(album)" title="The Number of the Beast (album)">The Number of the Beast</a></i>, which gave the band their first UK Albums Chart No.&#160;1 record<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004227_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004227-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and additionally became a Top Ten hit in many other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004228_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004228-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> At the time, Dickinson was in the midst of legal difficulties with Samson's management, and was not permitted to add his name to any of the songwriting credits, although he still made what he described as a "moral contribution" to "Children of the Damned", "The Prisoner" and "<a href="/wiki/Run_to_the_Hills" title="Run to the Hills">Run to the Hills</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShooman200782_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShooman200782-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> For the second time the band embarked on a world tour, dubbed <a href="/wiki/The_Beast_on_the_Road" title="The Beast on the Road">The Beast on the Road</a>, during which they visited North America, Japan, Australia, and Europe, including a headline appearance at the Reading Festival. A new and hugely successful chapter in Iron Maiden's future was cemented; in 2010 <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported that the album had sold over 14 million copies worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPfanner2010_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPfanner2010-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Beast on the Road's US leg proved controversial when an American conservative political lobbying group claimed that Iron Maiden were <a href="/wiki/Satanism" title="Satanism">Satanic</a> because of the new album's title track,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004228_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004228-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> to the point where a group of Christian <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">activists</a> destroyed Iron Maiden records as a protest against the band.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung(1)_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung(1)-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In recent years, Dickinson stated that the band treated this as "silliness",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEagle_Vision2001_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEagle_Vision2001-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and that the demonstrations in fact gave them "loads of publicity".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEMI2004_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEMI2004-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg/170px-Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg/255px-Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg/340px-Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicko_McBrain_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nicko_McBrain" title="Nicko McBrain">Nicko McBrain</a> has been Iron Maiden's drummer since 1982</div></div></div>
<p>In December 1982, drummer <a href="/wiki/Clive_Burr" title="Clive Burr">Clive Burr</a> was fired from the band and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Nicko_McBrain" title="Nicko McBrain">Nicko McBrain</a>, who previously played for <a href="/wiki/Trust_(French_band)" title="Trust (French band)">Trust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004233_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004233-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Although Harris stated that his dismissal took place because his live performances were affected by offstage activities,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004232–233_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004232–233-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Burr objected to this, and claimed that he was unfairly ousted from the band.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarlow2011_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarlow2011-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Soon afterwards, the band journeyed for the first time to The Bahamas to record the first of three consecutive albums at <a href="/wiki/Compass_Point_Studios" title="Compass Point Studios">Compass Point Studios</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004246_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004246-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In 1983, they released their fourth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Piece_of_Mind" title="Piece of Mind">Piece of Mind</a></i>, which reached the No.&#160;3 spot in the UK,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004247_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004247-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and was the band's debut in the North American charts, reaching No.&#160;70 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillboard_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillboard-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <i>Piece of Mind</i> includes the successful singles, "<a href="/wiki/The_Trooper" title="The Trooper">The Trooper</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Flight_of_Icarus" title="Flight of Icarus">Flight of Icarus</a>", the latter of which being particularly notable as one of the band's few songs to gain substantial airplay in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004245_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004245-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Soon after the success of <i>Piece of Mind</i> and its <a href="/wiki/World_Piece_Tour" title="World Piece Tour">supporting tour</a>, the band released their fifth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Powerslave" title="Powerslave">Powerslave</a></i>, on 9 September 1984. The album features the singles "<a href="/wiki/2_Minutes_to_Midnight" title="2 Minutes to Midnight">2 Minutes to Midnight</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Aces_High_(song)" title="Aces High (song)">Aces High</a>", as well as "Rime of The Ancient Mariner", based on <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Rime_of_the_Ancient_Mariner" class="mw-redirect" title="Rime of the Ancient Mariner">poem of the same name</a>, and running over 13 minutes in length.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004253_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004253-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The tour, following the album, dubbed the <a href="/wiki/World_Slavery_Tour" title="World Slavery Tour">World Slavery Tour</a>, was the band's largest to date, and consisted of 193 shows in 28 countries over 13 months,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004253_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004253-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> playing to an estimated 3,500,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStenning2006102_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStenning2006102-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Many shows were played back-to-back in the same city, such as in Long Beach, California, where the band played four consecutive concerts. Their first live album, <i><a href="/wiki/Live_After_Death" title="Live After Death">Live After Death</a></i>, was recorded, which became a critical and commercial success, peaking at No.&#160;4 in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004257_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004257-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Iron Maiden also made their debut appearance in South America, where they co-headlined (with <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a>) the <a href="/wiki/Rock_in_Rio" title="Rock in Rio">Rock in Rio</a> festival to an estimated 300,000 in attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEMI2008_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEMI2008-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The tour was physically gruelling for the band, who demanded six months off when it ended (although this was later reduced to four months).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004258_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004258-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> This was the first substantial break in the group's history, including the cancellation of a proposed supporting tour for the new live album,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStenning2006104_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStenning2006104-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> with Bruce Dickinson threatening to quit unless the tour ended.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004255_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004255-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Experimentation_.281986.E2.80.931989.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimentation_(1986–1989)">Experimentation (1986–1989)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Experimentation (1986–1989)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Somewhere_in_Time_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Somewhere in Time (Iron Maiden album)">Somewhere in Time (Iron Maiden album)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Son_of_a_Seventh_Son" title="Seventh Son of a Seventh Son">Seventh Son of a Seventh Son</a></div>
<p>Returning from their time off, the band adopted a different style for their 1986 studio album, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Somewhere_in_Time_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Somewhere in Time (Iron Maiden album)">Somewhere in Time</a></i>, featuring, for the first time in the band's history, <a href="/wiki/Synthesiser" class="mw-redirect" title="Synthesiser">synthesised</a> bass and guitars to add textures and layers to the sound.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuey(3)_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuey(3)-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The release charted well across the world, particularly with the single "<a href="/wiki/Wasted_Years" title="Wasted Years">Wasted Years</a>", but notably included no writing credits from Dickinson, whose material was rejected by the rest of the band.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004260_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004260-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> While Dickinson was focused on his own music, guitarist Adrian Smith, who typically collaborated with the vocalist, was "left to [his] own devices" and began writing songs on his own, coming up with "Wasted Years", "Sea of Madness", and "<a href="/wiki/Stranger_in_a_Strange_Land_(Iron_Maiden_song)" title="Stranger in a Strange Land (Iron Maiden song)">Stranger in a Strange Land</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004261_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004261-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> the last of which would be the album's second single.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004260_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004260-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The experimentation evident on <i>Somewhere in Time</i> continued on their next album, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Seventh_Son_of_a_Seventh_Son" title="Seventh Son of a Seventh Son">Seventh Son of a Seventh Son</a></i>, which was released in 1988. A <a href="/wiki/Concept_album" title="Concept album">concept album</a>, based on the 1987 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Seventh_Son_(novel)" title="Seventh Son (novel)">Seventh Son</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Orson_Scott_Card" title="Orson Scott Card">Orson Scott Card</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopoff2005(d)_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopoff2005(d)-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> would be the band's first record to include keyboards, performed by Harris and Smith,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopoff2005(d)_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopoff2005(d)-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Guitar_synthesizer" title="Guitar synthesizer">guitar synthesisers</a> on the previous release.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004265_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004265-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> After his contributions were not used for <i>Somewhere in Time</i>, Dickinson's enthusiasm was renewed as his ideas were accepted for this album.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004265_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004265-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Another popular release, it became Iron Maiden's second album to hit No.&#160;1 in the UK charts,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004263_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004263-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> although it only achieved a Gold certification in the US, in contrast to its four predecessors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERIAA_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERIAA-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the following <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Tour_of_a_Seventh_Tour" title="Seventh Tour of a Seventh Tour">tour</a>, the band headlined the <a href="/wiki/Monsters_of_Rock" title="Monsters of Rock">Monsters of Rock</a> festival at <a href="/wiki/Donington_Park" title="Donington Park">Donington Park</a> for the first time on 20 August 1988, playing to the largest crowd in the festival's history, with an estimated 107,000 in attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004269_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004269-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Also included on the bill were <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Lee_Roth" title="David Lee Roth">David Lee Roth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megadeth" title="Megadeth">Megadeth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N&#39; Roses">Guns N' Roses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helloween" title="Helloween">Helloween</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUK_Rock_Festivals_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUK_Rock_Festivals-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The festival was marred, however, by the deaths of two fans in a crowd-surge during the aforementioned Guns N' Roses performance; the following year's festival was cancelled as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004269_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004269-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The tour concluded with several headline shows in the UK in November and December 1988, with the concerts at the <a href="/wiki/National_Exhibition_Centre" title="National Exhibition Centre">NEC Arena</a>, Birmingham recorded for a live video, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Maiden_England" title="Maiden England">Maiden England</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004272_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004272-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the tour, Harris' bass technician, Michael Kenney, provided live keyboards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004266Johnson1988_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004266Johnson1988-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Kenney has acted as the band's live keyboard player ever since, also performing on the band's four following albums before Harris took over as the group's sole studio keyboardist from 2000's <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Brave New World (Iron Maiden album)">Brave New World</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGennet2010_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGennet2010-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Upheaval_.281989.E2.80.931994.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Upheaval_(1989–1994)">Upheaval (1989–1994)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Upheaval (1989–1994)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/No_Prayer_for_the_Dying" title="No Prayer for the Dying">No Prayer for the Dying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fear_of_the_Dark_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Fear of the Dark (Iron Maiden album)">Fear of the Dark (Iron Maiden album)</a></div>
<p>During another break in 1989, guitarist Adrian Smith released a solo album with his band <a href="/wiki/ASAP_(band)" title="ASAP (band)">ASAP</a>, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Silver_and_Gold_(ASAP_album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver and Gold (ASAP album)">Silver and Gold</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004273_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004273-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and vocalist Bruce Dickinson began work on a solo album with former <a href="/wiki/Gillan_(band)" title="Gillan (band)">Gillan</a> guitarist <a href="/wiki/Janick_Gers" title="Janick Gers">Janick Gers</a>, releasing <i><a href="/wiki/Tattooed_Millionaire" title="Tattooed Millionaire">Tattooed Millionaire</a></i> in 1990,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004281_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004281-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> followed by a tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004285_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004285-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, to mark the band's ten-year recording anniversary, Iron Maiden released a compilation collection, <i><a href="/wiki/The_First_Ten_Years_(boxed_set)" title="The First Ten Years (boxed set)">The First Ten Years</a></i>, a series of ten CDs and double <a href="/wiki/12-inch_single" class="mw-redirect" title="12-inch single">12-inch singles</a>. Between 24 February and 28 April 1990, the individual parts were released one-by-one, each containing two of Iron Maiden's singles, including the original B-sides.
</p><p>Soon afterwards, Iron Maiden regrouped to work on a new studio record. During the pre-production stages, Adrian Smith left the band due to differences with Steve Harris, regarding the direction the band should be taking, disagreeing with the "stripped down" style that they were leaning towards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004283_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004283-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Janick Gers, having worked on Dickinson's solo project, was chosen to replace Smith, and became the band's first new member in seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004285_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004285-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The album, <i><a href="/wiki/No_Prayer_for_the_Dying" title="No Prayer for the Dying">No Prayer for the Dying</a></i>, was released in October 1990,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrato_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrato-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and contained "<a href="/wiki/Bring_Your_Daughter..._to_the_Slaughter" title="Bring Your Daughter... to the Slaughter">Bring Your Daughter... to the Slaughter</a>", the band's first (and to date, only) <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">UK Singles Chart</a> No.&#160;1, originally recorded by Dickinson's solo outfit for the soundtrack to <i><a href="/wiki/A_Nightmare_on_Elm_Street_5:_The_Dream_Child" title="A Nightmare on Elm Street 5: The Dream Child">A Nightmare on Elm Street 5: The Dream Child</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004282_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004282-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The song was banned by the BBC and only a 90-second live clip on Top of the Pops was shown.
</p><p>After another <a href="/wiki/No_Prayer_on_the_Road" title="No Prayer on the Road">tour</a> and some more time off, the band recorded their next studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Fear_of_the_Dark_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Fear of the Dark (Iron Maiden album)">Fear of the Dark</a></i>, which was released in 1992 and included the stand-out <a href="/wiki/Fear_of_the_Dark_(song)" title="Fear of the Dark (song)">title track</a>, which is now a regular fixture in the band's concert setlists. Achieving their third No.&#160;1 in the UK albums chart,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004289_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004289-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> the disc also featured the No.&#160;2 single "<a href="/wiki/Be_Quick_or_Be_Dead" title="Be Quick or Be Dead">Be Quick or Be Dead</a>" and the No.&#160;21 single "<a href="/wiki/From_Here_to_Eternity_(Iron_Maiden_song)" title="From Here to Eternity (Iron Maiden song)">From Here to Eternity</a>". The album featured the first songwriting by Gers, and no collaboration at all between Harris and Dickinson on songs. The <a href="/wiki/Fear_of_the_Dark_Tour" title="Fear of the Dark Tour">extensive worldwide tour that followed</a> included their first ever Latin American leg (after a single concert during the World Slavery Tour), and headlining the <a href="/wiki/Monsters_of_Rock" title="Monsters of Rock">Monsters of Rock</a> festivals in seven European countries. Iron Maiden's second performance at <a href="/wiki/Donington_Park" title="Donington Park">Donington Park</a>, to an audience of 68,500 (the attendance was capped after the incident in 1988),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004291_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004291-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> was filmed for the audio and video release, <i><a href="/wiki/Live_at_Donington_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Live at Donington (Iron Maiden album)">Live at Donington</a></i>, and featured a guest appearance by Adrian Smith, who joined the band to perform "Running Free".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004291_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004291-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1993, Dickinson left the band to further pursue his solo career, but agreed to remain for a <a href="/wiki/Real_Live_Tour" title="Real Live Tour">farewell tour</a> and two live albums (later re-released in <a href="/wiki/A_Real_Live_Dead_One" title="A Real Live Dead One">one package</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004293_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004293-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The first, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Real_Live_One" title="A Real Live One">A Real Live One</a></i>, featured songs from 1986 to 1992, and was released in March 1993. The second, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Real_Dead_One" title="A Real Dead One">A Real Dead One</a></i>, featured songs from 1980 to 1984, and was released after Dickinson had left the band. The tour did not go well, with Steve Harris claiming that Dickinson would only perform properly for high-profile shows, and that at several concerts, he would only mumble into the microphone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004296_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004296-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Dickinson denied that he was under-performing, stating that it was impossible to "make like Mr Happy Face if the vibe wasn't right", saying that news of his exit from the band had prevented any chance of a good atmosphere during the tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004297_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004297-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> He played his farewell show with Iron Maiden on 28 August 1993, which was filmed, broadcast by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>, and released on video under the name <i><a href="/wiki/Raising_Hell_(video)" title="Raising Hell (video)">Raising Hell</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004298_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004298-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Blaze_Bayley_era.2C_The_X_Factor_and_Virtual_XI_.281994.E2.80.931999.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Blaze_Bayley_era,_The_X_Factor_and_Virtual_XI_(1994–1999)">Blaze Bayley era, <i>The X Factor</i> and <i>Virtual XI</i> (1994–1999)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Blaze Bayley era, The X Factor and Virtual XI (1994–1999)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(album)" title="The X Factor (album)">The X Factor (album)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virtual_XI" title="Virtual XI">Virtual XI</a></div>
<p>In 1994, the band listened to hundreds of tapes sent in by vocalists before convincing <a href="/wiki/Blaze_Bayley" title="Blaze Bayley">Blaze Bayley</a>, formerly of the band <a href="/wiki/Wolfsbane_(band)" title="Wolfsbane (band)">Wolfsbane</a>, who had supported Iron Maiden in 1990, to audition for them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004301_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004301-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Harris' preferred choice from the outset,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004302_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004302-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Bayley had a different vocal style from his predecessor, which ultimately received a mixed reception among fans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStagno2006(b)_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStagno2006(b)-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After a two-year hiatus (as well as a three-year hiatus from studio releases – a record for the band at the time), Iron Maiden returned in 1995. Releasing their next studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(album)" title="The X Factor (album)">The X Factor</a></i>, the band had their lowest chart position since 1981 for an album in the UK (debuting at No.&#160;8),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004313_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004313-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> although it would go on to win <i>Album of the Year</i> awards in France and Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004311_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004311-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>  The record included the 11-minute epic "Sign of the Cross", the band's longest song since "Rime of the Ancient Mariner", as well as the singles, "<a href="/wiki/Man_on_the_Edge" title="Man on the Edge">Man on the Edge</a>", based on the film <i><a href="/wiki/Falling_Down" title="Falling Down">Falling Down</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaterson200944_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaterson200944-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and "<a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Flies_(song)" title="Lord of the Flies (song)">Lord of the Flies</a>", based on the <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Lord of the Flies">novel of the same name</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopoff2005(b)_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopoff2005(b)-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The release is notable for its "dark" tone, inspired by Steve Harris' divorce.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004311_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004311-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_X_Factour" title="The X Factour">The band toured</a> for the rest of 1995 and 1996, playing for the first time in Israel and South Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004314_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004314-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and ending in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. After the tour, Iron Maiden released a compilation album, <i><a href="/wiki/Best_of_the_Beast" title="Best of the Beast">Best of the Beast</a></i>. The band's first compilation, it included a new single, "<a href="/wiki/Virus_(Iron_Maiden_song)" title="Virus (Iron Maiden song)">Virus</a>", in which the lyrics attack the critics, who had recently written off the band.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004316_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004316-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998, Iron Maiden released <i><a href="/wiki/Virtual_XI" title="Virtual XI">Virtual XI</a></i>, whose chart scores were the band's lowest to date, failing to score one million worldwide sales for the first time in the band's history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStagno2006(a)_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStagno2006(a)-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESverigetopplistan_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESverigetopplistan-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The album peaked at No.&#160;16 in the UK; the band's lowest for a new studio record.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOfficial_Charts_Company1998_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOfficial_Charts_Company1998-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>  At the same time, Steve Harris assisted in remastering the band's entire discography, up to and including <i>Live at Donington</i> (which was given a mainstream release for the first time).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaulnier2010_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaulnier2010-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bayley's tenure in Iron Maiden ended in January 1999 when he was asked to leave during a band meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004324_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004324-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The dismissal took place due to issues Bayley had experienced with his voice during the <a href="/wiki/Virtual_XI_World_Tour" title="Virtual XI World Tour">Virtual XI World Tour</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004321_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004321-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> although Janick Gers stated that this was partly the band's fault for forcing him to perform songs, which were beyond his natural register.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrave_Words_&amp;_Bloody_Knuckles2010_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrave_Words_&amp;_Bloody_Knuckles2010-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Return_of_Dickinson_and_Smith.2C_Brave_New_World_.281999.E2.80.932002.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_of_Dickinson_and_Smith,_Brave_New_World_(1999–2002)">Return of Dickinson and Smith, <i>Brave New World</i> (1999–2002)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Return of Dickinson and Smith, Brave New World (1999–2002)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ed_Hunter" title="Ed Hunter">Ed Hunter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Brave New World (Iron Maiden album)">Brave New World (Iron Maiden album)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg/220px-Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg/330px-Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg/440px-Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_en_Costa_Rica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adrian Smith (left) re-joined Iron Maiden in 1999, resulting in a three guitar line-up.</div></div></div>
<p>While the group were considering a replacement for Bayley, Rod Smallwood convinced Steve Harris to invite Bruce Dickinson back into the band.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004328_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004328-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Although Harris admitted that he "wasn't really into it" at first, he then thought, "'Well, if the change happens, who should we get?' The thing is, we know Bruce and we know what he's capable of, and you think, 'Well, better the devil you know.' I mean, we got on well professionally for, like, eleven years, and so&#160;... after I thought about it, I didn't really have a problem with it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004328_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004328-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The band entered into talks with Dickinson, who agreed to rejoin during a meeting in Brighton in January 1999,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004329_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004329-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> along with guitarist Adrian Smith, who was telephoned a few hours later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004330_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004330-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> With Gers, Smith's replacement, remaining, Iron Maiden now had a three-guitar line-up, and embarked on a hugely successful reunion tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004331_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004331-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Dubbed <a href="/wiki/The_Ed_Hunter_Tour" title="The Ed Hunter Tour">The Ed Hunter Tour</a>, it tied in with the band's newly released greatest hits collection, <i><a href="/wiki/Ed_Hunter" title="Ed Hunter">Ed Hunter</a></i>, whose track listing was decided by a poll on the group's website, and also contained a computer game of the same name starring <a href="/wiki/Eddie_(mascot)" title="Eddie (mascot)">the band's mascot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Dickinson's primary concerns on rejoining the group "was whether we would in fact be making a real state-of-the-art record and not just a comeback album,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004328_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004328-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> which eventually took the form of 2000's <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Brave New World (Iron Maiden album)">Brave New World</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004341_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004341-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Having disliked the results from Harris' personal studio, Barnyard Studios located on his property in Essex,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerelian2000_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerelian2000-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> which had been used for the last four Iron Maiden studio albums, the band recorded the new release at Guillaume Tell Studios in Paris, France in November 1999 with producer <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Shirley" title="Kevin Shirley">Kevin Shirley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004341_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004341-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Thematic influences continued with "<a href="/wiki/The_Wicker_Man_(song)" title="The Wicker Man (song)">The Wicker Man</a>" – based on the 1973 British <a href="/wiki/The_Wicker_Man_(1973_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Wicker Man (1973 film)">cult film of the same name</a> – and "Brave New World" – title taken from the <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a> <a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World">novel of the same name</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004342_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004342-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The album furthered the more progressive and melodic sound present in some earlier recordings, with elaborate song structures and keyboard orchestration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004342_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004342-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World_Tour" title="Brave New World Tour">The world tour</a> that followed consisted of well over 100&#160;dates and culminated on 19 January 2001 in a show at the <a href="/wiki/Rock_in_Rio" title="Rock in Rio">Rock in Rio</a> festival in Brazil, where Iron Maiden played to an audience of around 250,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004353Shooman2007176Sanctuary2002Martins2002_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004353Shooman2007176Sanctuary2002Martins2002-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> While the performance was being produced for a CD and DVD release in March 2002, under the name <i><a href="/wiki/Rock_in_Rio_(album)" title="Rock in Rio (album)">Rock in Rio</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004357_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004357-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> the band took a year off from touring, during which they played three consecutive shows at <a href="/wiki/Brixton_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Brixton Academy">Brixton Academy</a> in aid of former drummer <a href="/wiki/Clive_Burr" title="Clive Burr">Clive Burr</a>, who had recently announced that he had been diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">multiple sclerosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004361_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004361-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The band performed two further concerts for Burr's MS Trust Fund charity in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005c_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005c-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> and 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007c_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007c-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> before his death in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBBC_News2013_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC_News2013-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Dance_of_Death_and_A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_.282003.E2.80.932007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dance_of_Death_and_A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_(2003–2007)"><i>Dance of Death</i> and <i>A Matter of Life and Death</i> (2003–2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dance of Death and A Matter of Life and Death (2003–2007)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dance_of_Death_(album)" title="Dance of Death (album)">Dance of Death (album)</a> and <a href="/wiki/A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_(album)" title="A Matter of Life and Death (album)">A Matter of Life and Death (album)</a></div>
<p>Following their <a href="/wiki/Give_Me_Ed..._%27Til_I%27m_Dead_Tour" title="Give Me Ed... &#39;Til I&#39;m Dead Tour">Give Me Ed... 'Til I'm Dead Tour</a> in the summer of 2003, Iron Maiden released <i><a href="/wiki/Dance_of_Death_(album)" title="Dance of Death (album)">Dance of Death</a></i>, their thirteenth studio album, which was met by worldwide critical and commercial success.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004368_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004368-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Produced by Kevin Shirley, now the band's regular producer, many critics also felt that this release matched up to their earlier efforts, such as <i>Killers</i>, <i>Piece of Mind</i> and <i>The Number of the Beast</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004369_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004369-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> As usual, historical, and literary references were present, with "Montségur" in particular being about the <a href="/wiki/Catharism" title="Catharism">Cathar</a> stronghold conquered in 1244,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004373_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004373-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and "Paschendale" relating to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="Battle of Passchendaele">the significant battle</a> which took place during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">the First World War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWall2004375_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWall2004375-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Dance_of_Death_World_Tour" title="Dance of Death World Tour">following tour</a>, the band's performance at <a href="/wiki/Westfalenhalle" class="mw-redirect" title="Westfalenhalle">Westfalenhalle</a>, in Dortmund, Germany, was recorded and released in August 2005 as a live album and DVD, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Death_on_the_Road" title="Death on the Road">Death on the Road</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005a_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005a-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, the band announced the <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Rips_Up_the_World_Tour" title="Eddie Rips Up the World Tour">Eddie Rips Up the World Tour</a>, which, tying in with their 2004 DVD entitled <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Iron_Maiden_%E2%80%93_Part_1:_The_Early_Days" title="The History of Iron Maiden – Part 1: The Early Days">The History of Iron Maiden – Part 1: The Early Days</a></i>, only featured material from their first four albums.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005b_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005b-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> As part of this celebration of their earlier years, "The Number of the Beast" single was re-released<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2004d_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2004d-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and went straight to No.&#160;3 in the UK Chart.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOfficial_Charts_Company2005_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOfficial_Charts_Company2005-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The tour included many headlining stadium and festival dates, including a performance at <a href="/wiki/Ullevi_Stadium" class="mw-redirect" title="Ullevi Stadium">Ullevi Stadium</a> in Sweden to an audience of almost 60,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetal_Underground2004_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetal_Underground2004-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> This concert was also broadcast live on satellite television all over Europe to approximately 60 million viewers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetal_Underground2005_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetal_Underground2005-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Following this run of European shows, the band co-headlined the US festival tour, <a href="/wiki/Ozzfest" title="Ozzfest">Ozzfest</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a>, their final performance at which earned international press coverage after their show was sabotaged by singer <a href="/wiki/Ozzy_Osbourne" title="Ozzy Osbourne">Ozzy Osbourne</a>'s family,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKNAC2005Sullivan2005_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKNAC2005Sullivan2005-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> who took offence to Dickinson's remarks against reality-TV.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilde2008_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilde2008-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The band completed the tour by headlining the <a href="/wiki/Reading_and_Leeds_Festivals" title="Reading and Leeds Festivals">Reading and Leeds Festivals</a> on the 26–28 August,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENME2005Williams2005_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENME2005Williams2005-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/RDS_Arena" title="RDS Arena">RDS Stadium</a> in Ireland on 31 August. For the second time, the band played a charity show for The Clive Burr MS Trust Fund, which took place at the <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith_Apollo" title="Hammersmith Apollo">Hammersmith Apollo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005c_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2005c-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The same year, the band were inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Guitar_Center#Hollywood&#39;s_RockWalk" title="Guitar Center">Hollywood RockWalk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Boulevard" title="Sunset Boulevard">Sunset Boulevard</a>, Los Angeles.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:209px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg/207px-Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg" decoding="async" width="207" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg/311px-Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg/414px-Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="819" data-file-height="614" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bruce_Dickinson_and_Eddie_30nov2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vocalist <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Dickinson" title="Bruce Dickinson">Bruce Dickinson</a> during A Matter of Life and Death World Tour. Throughout the tour's first leg, the band played the <i>A Matter of Life and Death</i> album in its entirety.</div></div></div>
<p>At the end of 2005, Iron Maiden began work on <i><a href="/wiki/A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_(album)" title="A Matter of Life and Death (album)">A Matter of Life and Death</a></i>, their fourteenth studio album, released in autumn 2006. While not a concept album,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2006_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2006-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> war and religion are recurring themes in the lyrics, as well as in the cover artwork. The release was a critical and commercial success, earning the band their first top ten in the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillboard2006_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillboard2006-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> and receiving the Album of the Year award at the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Classic_Rock_(magazine)" title="Classic Rock (magazine)"><i>Classic Rock</i> Roll of Honour Awards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2006b_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2006b-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_Tour" title="A Matter of Life and Death Tour">supporting tour</a> followed, during which they played the album in its entirety; response to this was mixed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVincentelli2006Evening_Times2006_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVincentelli2006Evening_Times2006-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The second part of the "A Matter of Life and Death" tour, which took place in 2007, was dubbed "A Matter of the Beast" to celebrate the 25th anniversary of <i>The Number of the Beast</i> album, and included appearances at several major festivals worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007b_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007b-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The tour opened in the Middle East with the band's first performance in Dubai at the <a href="/wiki/Dubai_Desert_Rock_Festival" title="Dubai Desert Rock Festival">Dubai Desert Rock Festival</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007a_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007a-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> after which they played to over 30,000&#160;people at the <a href="/wiki/Bangalore_Palace" title="Bangalore Palace">Bangalore Palace</a> Grounds,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVulliamy2007_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVulliamy2007-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> marking the first concert by any major heavy metal band in the Indian sub-continent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007a_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007a-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The band went on to play a string of European dates, including an appearance at <a href="/wiki/Download_Festival" title="Download Festival">Download Festival</a>, their fourth headline performance at <a href="/wiki/Donington_Park" title="Donington Park">Donington Park</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetal_Underground2007_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetal_Underground2007-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> to approximately 80,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007d_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007d-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> On 24 June they ended the tour with a performance at London's <a href="/wiki/Brixton_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Brixton Academy">Brixton Academy</a> in aid of The Clive Burr <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">MS</a> Trust fund.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007c_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlabbermouth.net2007c-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Somewhere_Back_in_Time_World_Tour_and_Flight_666_.282007.E2.80.932009.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Somewhere_Back_in_Time_World_Tour_and_Flight_666_(2007–2009)">Somewhere Back in Time World Tour and <i>Flight 666</i> (2007–2009)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_Maiden&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Somewhere Back in Time World Tour and Flight 666 (2007–2009)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Somewhere_Back_in_Time_World_Tour" title="Somewhere Back in Time World Tour">Somewhere Back in Time World Tour</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden:_Flight_666" title="Iron Maiden: Flight 666">Iron Maiden: Flight 666</a></div>
<p>On 5 September 2007, the band announced their <a href="/wiki/Somewhere_Back_in_Time_World_Tour" title="Somewhere Back in Time World Tour">Somewhere Back in Time World Tour</a>, which tied in with the DVD release of their <i>Live After Death</i> album.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2007_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2007-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The setlist for the tour consisted of successes from the 1980s, with a specific emphasis on the <i>Powerslave</i> era for set design.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2007_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2007-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The first part of the tour, commencing in Mumbai, India on 1 February 2008, consisted of 24 concerts in 21 cities, travelling nearly 50,000 miles in the band's own chartered aeroplane,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetal_Storm2007_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetal_Storm2007-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> named "Ed Force One".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECashmere2008_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECashmere2008-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> They played their first ever concerts in Costa Rica and Colombia and their first shows in Australia and Puerto Rico since 1992.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_086.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Iron_Maiden_086.jpg/220px-Iron_Maiden_086.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Iron_Maiden_086.jpg/330px-Iron_Maiden_086.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Iron_Maiden_086.jpg/440px-Iron_Maiden_086.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_086.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iron Maiden performing in Toronto during the Somewhere Back in Time World Tour 2008. The stage set largely emulated that of the World Slavery Tour 1984–85.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2007_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2007-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The tour led to the release of a new compilation album, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Somewhere_Back_in_Time" title="Somewhere Back in Time">Somewhere Back in Time</a></i>, which included a selection of tracks from their 1980 eponymous debut to 1988's <i>Seventh Son of a Seventh Son</i>, as well as several live versions from <i>Live After Death</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2008_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2008-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
